<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2376876954600102206">In 1967 in Greenville, Mississippi, known Klan member Sam Cayhall is accused of bombing the law offices of Jewish civil rights activist Marvin Kramer, killing Kramer's two sons. Cayhall's first trial, with an all-white jury and a Klan rally outside the courthouse, ends in a hung jury; the retrial six months later has the same outcome.<br /><br />Twelve years later an ambitions distric</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2376876954600102206">In 1967 in Greenville, Mississippi, known Klan member Sam Cayhall is accused of bombing the law offices of Jewish civil rights activist Marvin Kramer, killing Kramer's two sons. Cayhall's first trial, with an all-white jury and a Klan rally outside the courthouse, ends in a hung jury; the retrial six months later has the same outcome.<br /><br />Twelve years later an ambitions district attorney in Greenville reopens the case. Much has changed since 1967, and this time, with a jury of eight whites and four blacks, Cayhall is convicted. He is transferred to the state penitentiary at Parchman to await execution on death row.<br /><br />In 1990, in the huge Chicago law firm of Kravitz &amp; Bane, a young laywer named Adam Hall asked to work on the Cayhall case, which the firm has handled on a pro bono basis for years. But the case is all but lost and time is running out: within weeks Sam Cayhall will finally go to the gas chamber. Why in the world would Adam want to get involved?</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2376876954600102206">...more</a>

      </div>

  <a href="/user/show/15139865-neja" class="left imgcol" title="Neja"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1439072145p2/15139865.jpg" alt="Neja" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1144470358?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 20, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/15139865-neja" class="user" title="Neja">Neja</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/5355.The_Chamber" title="Paperback 9780385339667" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1144470358">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2653528785881162358">I started reading this book in December. Why did it took me so long to finish it? Because of the topic: death row, KKK, racism plus I saw the movie before reading it. I am strongly against death row and I can't believe something so inhumane can be legal. These things are so horrible and painful to read and because of that I've read it only a couple of times a month. It made me feel so sad every time I read it. It's a great book, amazing writing, but really sad. You can't read it with a dry eye.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2653528785881162358">I started reading this book in December. Why did it took me so long to finish it? Because of the topic: death row, KKK, racism plus I saw the movie before reading it. I am strongly against death row and I can't believe something so inhumane can be legal. These things are so horrible and painful to read and because of that I've read it only a couple of times a month. It made me feel so sad every time I read it. It's a great book, amazing writing, but really sad. You can't read it with a dry eye. I didn't.</span>

      <a href="/user/show/1648274-julie" class="user" title="Julie">Julie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/5355.The_Chamber" title="Paperback 9780385339667" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1648274-julie?shelf=my-favourites" class="actionLinkLite">my-favourites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer40565844">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6610556552815357599">I thought this was one of the most impacting novels I have read for a long time. Grisham thoroughly explores the implications of the death penalty and creates a well-rounded and complex character in Sam Cayhall. You come to hate him and pity him, asking yourself whether he really deserves death and cheering Adam on as he tries to save him. A book I will never forget.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_431350441">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/431350441" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/8823337-shesh" class="left imgcol" title="Shesh"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1334606355p2/8823337.jpg" alt="Shesh" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/431350441?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 10, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8823337-shesh" class="user" title="Shesh">Shesh</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5355.The_Chamber" title="Paperback 9780385339667" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer431350441">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12967698620771653007">Just finished rereading this amazing book. This isn't one of Grisham's more popular stories however this book was a life transforming experience for me. Two issues this book forced me to deal with on my first reading in 1994: 1) How can people - any people, "Cluckers" (KKK), the Taliban, street gangs,Fred Phelps and congregation, boy soldiers of Sierra Leone, contract killers, even bullies, et al - be so cruel and mean and hateful? Where is compassion? How did they miss that piece of life? How c</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12967698620771653007">Just finished rereading this amazing book. This isn't one of Grisham's more popular stories however this book was a life transforming experience for me. Two issues this book forced me to deal with on my first reading in 1994: 1) How can people - any people, "Cluckers" (KKK), the Taliban, street gangs,Fred Phelps and congregation, boy soldiers of Sierra Leone, contract killers, even bullies, et al - be so cruel and mean and hateful? Where is compassion? How did they miss that piece of life? How could even members of my own family be so racist (all from Arkansas)? 2)Do I truly oppose the death penalty or do I just "tout" that I oppose the death penalty?<br /><br />Good to read this again and be reminded of my own compassion and convictions. Pps 400-401 are the magic ones for me. Adam looking at the picture of Sam, his grandfather at 15, celebrating the lynching of a black man with neighbors and family - "He studied the clear, beautiful eyes of his grandfather and his heart ached. He was just a boy, born and reared in a household where hatred of blacks and others was simply a way of life. How much of it could be blamed on him? Look at those around him, his father, family, friends and neighbors, all probably honest, poor, hardworking people caught for the moment at the end of a cruel ceremony that was commonplace in their society. Sam didn't have a chance. This was the only world he knew. . . . would Adam have been right there in the middle of them if he had been born forty years earlier? . . .How is God's world could Sam Cayhall have become anything other than himself?"<br /><br />Certainly that is not the answer in every circumstance - there is still the nature or nurture question. But this book cemented my own understanding of "situatedness" and has informed my compassionate self, assisted in transforming my spiritual sense to a place of understanding. Not every time, of course, but often.<br /><br />And the death penalty? Absolutely not. No gas chamber, no lethal injection, no firing squad. However, I always add this caveat: I have never had a loved one who has been a victim of a capital crime. I would hope that my convictions would remain if that were ever the case.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12967698620771653007">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9812272678163744354">Typically I am a Sci-fi/Fantasy reader, with a smattering of historical fiction thrown in. Really I'll read just about anything, but I have weakness for fairy tales. So when my dad recommended this book to me, and went as far as to buy it and give it to me, I was like... okay... But I decided to give it a chance because it's a book, and I like books.<br /><br />And my response was: Wow. I have read very few things as heart stirring and thought provoking as this book. Who could like a KKK member? Murdering p</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9812272678163744354">Typically I am a Sci-fi/Fantasy reader, with a smattering of historical fiction thrown in. Really I'll read just about anything, but I have weakness for fairy tales. So when my dad recommended this book to me, and went as far as to buy it and give it to me, I was like... okay... But I decided to give it a chance because it's a book, and I like books.<br /><br />And my response was: Wow. I have read very few things as heart stirring and thought provoking as this book. Who could like a KKK member? Murdering people is ALWAYS bad, right? And shouldn't people who murder people die? Before I read this book I would have said not me, yes, and yes, to each of those questions (respectively). Now though? I would say it depends, yes, and I don't know. I learned a lot about humanity. I also learned that the best ending isn't always the happiest one. Wow. This book is good. No matter what your typical genre is, take a break and give this book a chance.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_91775716">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91775716" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1756815-wendell" class="left imgcol" title="Wendell"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442473020p2/1756815.jpg" alt="Wendell" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/91775716?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 28, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1756815-wendell" class="user" title="Wendell">Wendell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/233115.The_Chamber" title="Paperback 9780099179511" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1756815-wendell?shelf=trash-horror-guilty-pleasures" class="actionLinkLite">trash-horror-guilty-pleasures</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer91775716'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer91775716">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10064195859173352684">In some metafictional world, I suppose, the painfully slow pace of this novel could be seen as an intentional strategy meant to reproduce the agonizing waiting that is involved in ensuring that a condemned prisoner keeps his appointment with the death house, but the book’s slackness is much more likely a simple reflection of the enormous difficulty Grisham encountered in telling a story whose ending was inevitable and which was, thus, incapable of producing any genuine dramatic tension. As it is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10064195859173352684">In some metafictional world, I suppose, the painfully slow pace of this novel could be seen as an intentional strategy meant to reproduce the agonizing waiting that is involved in ensuring that a condemned prisoner keeps his appointment with the death house, but the book’s slackness is much more likely a simple reflection of the enormous difficulty Grisham encountered in telling a story whose ending was inevitable and which was, thus, incapable of producing any genuine dramatic tension. As it is, Grisham walks a thin line between depicting, on the one hand, the gruesome pitilessness of Sam Cayhall's crimes and the anguish of his victims and their families and, on the other, creating sympathy for the suffering of Cayhall and his own family (especially his grandson, Adam, the young lawyer who attempts to save Cayhall's life). In the end, the book comes down squarely against the death penalty, doing what it can to make clear that Cayhall’s crimes rippled far beyond his immediate victims, engulfing his own children and grandchildren in a legacy of suffering that permanently shapes their lives and threatens to have no end. (The demons, says Cayhall's daughter, Lee, in the book's final chapters, are never destroyed; they just go off to haunt someone else.) In that way, Grisham lets Cayhall stand as a symbol of the decades of hate in the South, the lynchings and racism, the crimes condoned, the horrors institutionalized, and his death is meant to suggest not that such an era has disappeared but rather to signal the end of a time in which no other choice was possible. “If I’d never heard of the Klan,” Cayhall declares in his final hours, “I’d be a free man today.” And so would the South, Grisham seems to suggest. At the conclusion of the novel, Grisham permits Adam and his aunt, Lee, to walk away with some small hope, but with their pain fully intact. Whether Cayhall’s death brought relief to his victims’ families or closed an agonizing chapter in Mississippi history, as the spineless, politically ambitious governor intones, remains an open question. Grisham nonetheless makes his own beliefs clear, and any reasonable reader would have difficulty discarding them: Cayhall’s execution changes nothing, resolves nothing, serves no tangible purpose. It is a last, useless act in the history of useless acts that Grisham traces. And perhaps that is what makes <em>The Chamber</em> such an utterly melancholy read.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10064195859173352684">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_171368810">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/171368810" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5515188-ryan" class="left imgcol" title="Ryan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1306460914p2/5515188.jpg" alt="Ryan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/171368810?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 26, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5515188-ryan" class="user" title="Ryan">Ryan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5355.The_Chamber" title="Paperback 9780385339667" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer171368810">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4473717323671904943"> Though I haven’t read this book entirely, it so far is actually quite captivating. As a whole, it has kept hold of my attention. It also has a hint of humor in it, too. John Grisham having a background in law has put his knowledge and experience in his books. <br /> “The Chamber” is about a young, bright lawyer, Adam Hall, who joined the Kravitz &amp; Bane firm solely to represent and defend his grandfather, the infamous ex-Klansman Sam Cayhall, who is in Death Row for the bombing of the Kramer Firm,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4473717323671904943"> Though I haven’t read this book entirely, it so far is actually quite captivating. As a whole, it has kept hold of my attention. It also has a hint of humor in it, too. John Grisham having a background in law has put his knowledge and experience in his books. <br />	“The Chamber” is about a young, bright lawyer, Adam Hall, who joined the Kravitz &amp; Bane firm solely to represent and defend his grandfather, the infamous ex-Klansman Sam Cayhall, who is in Death Row for the bombing of the Kramer Firm, killing two children. Sam Cayhall is due to be sent into the gas chamber for the two murders. Adam risks everything to give his grandfather another appeal and to learn more about his family’s dark history in the process. <br />	I was moved by Adam’s bravery and courage for exposing himself as the grandson of an infamous Klansman. While the other family members hide and disregard Sam as an embarrassment to the family, Adam comes out of hiding to save his grandfather. No matter how racist or horrible his grandfather was, he is still willing to fight to represent and get his grandfather out of Death Row and give him a second chance in life no matter the cost. 	<br />	I actually have two favorite parts. I liked the second chapter because it’s about when Adam reveals himself as one of his firm’s client’s long-lost grandson to his superiors at the firm and struggles to get the chance to represent his grandfather. My other favorite part is when Adam learns more about his family. He learns of a first cousin who fled to Europe from his aunt who is the daughter of Sam Cayhall.<br />	In my opinion, I think this one of his best books, because it shows the horrors of the death penalty. He really brings out in detail of the different death penalty devices in history and their horrible effect on the people they are made to kill.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4473717323671904943">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4664245819028659130"> I generally was not a John Grisham fan. I had read "Peligan Brief" many years ago and found it too detailed and too boring. This is not the way I felt about this book - so John Grisham, you have earned your way back into my reading library.<br /> The Gas Chamber is being prepared....time is running out - - Will this client be set free? John Grisham takes you through every emotion - - liking this client and even somewhat accepting perhaps of what he did - - to hating this guy and wanting to pull the gas line on him yourself.<br /> I found myself biting my nails as I got near the ending.....I almost felt time was running out for me! In the literature world everyone loves a 'happy' ending...which would mean the guy would get set free and live a forgiving life....but does he? Can you, while reading, come up with a solution as to why this guy should/should not be set free. He freely admist the crime...that is never the question....then what else is there to say? A lot!!! You'll find yourself grabbing every minute you can to read the next paragraph......</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=me-likey-a-lot" class="actionLinkLite">me-likey-a-lot</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=library-books" class="actionLinkLite">library-books</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer171232126">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17576119215658968721">It was, as most of Grisham's books, absolutely fantastic! I am amazed how he manages to make his books so much about the legal stuff of a lawyer's life and still put so much soul in it that it turns into some mind boggling story! The rythm of the book is fast, constantly coming up with new things, the action is complex and sometimes even sick, and the ending is amazing! <br />Though it treats legal problems, as I said before, he manages to make his way up to the human mind and study it at close range.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17576119215658968721">It was, as most of Grisham's books, absolutely fantastic! I am amazed how he manages to make his books so much about the legal stuff of a lawyer's life and still put so much soul in it that it turns into some mind boggling story! The rythm of the book is fast, constantly coming up with new things, the action is complex and sometimes even sick, and the ending is amazing! <br />Though it treats legal problems, as I said before, he manages to make his way up to the human mind and study it at close range. It's a story of a disturbed man (Sam) who has had a really messed up life, just to realize at the end that he is sorry for everything. His name is Sam and he is a Ku Klux Klan member, trying to eliminate black and jewish people. His nephew, Adam Hall, finds him in prison and tries to save him from the death penalty. He does everything he can to take his grandphater's life out of death's hands, and he sacrifices things and people on the way. <br />These two people are the important people in the book, but then others pop up and make the story even spicier : Adam's aunt, Lee, his father, Eddie (Sam's son) and other people that have no connection with his familly : Goodman, a lawyer, and others. These characters all come with different stories and personalities and make the story that much more interesting. <br /><br />All in all, John Grisham managed with this book to put the reader into somewhat a dilemma : is it right to kill a person just beacuse it, at it's turn, killed? </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17576119215658968721">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer169407106">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14956893117298406880">I've read a couple of Grishams that had very rich and exciting plots, only to end abruptly, leaving the reader wanting for more.<br /><br />I'm happy to say that that's not the case with The Chamber. This story has both the thrills and the heart and it carried through to the end. It was an exciting read, and tugs at the heart at the same time; I almost cried in some of the scenes.<br /><br />I admire the character of Adam Hall, I wish there were more people like him in real life - those who would put the good of the fa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14956893117298406880">I've read a couple of Grishams that had very rich and exciting plots, only to end abruptly, leaving the reader wanting for more.<br /><br />I'm happy to say that that's not the case with The Chamber. This story has both the thrills and the heart and it carried through to the end. It was an exciting read, and tugs at the heart at the same time; I almost cried in some of the scenes.<br /><br />I admire the character of Adam Hall, I wish there were more people like him in real life - those who would put the good of the family first, no matter what the price. I also admire the character of Sam Cayhall. Observers would say he deserved what he got (and maybe he did, who can really tell), but only he would know that he had made peace with himself and with God somehow before his end; and Sam did not see the need for this to be publicized. It's a sad story, but there was redemption at the end. Something good was borne out of something bad; and that's not a lesson to be ignored.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_822849160">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/822849160" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/17986674-ross" class="left imgcol" title="Ross"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1362269290p2/17986674.jpg" alt="Ross" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/822849160?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 13, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/17986674-ross" class="user" title="Ross">Ross</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5355.The_Chamber" title="Paperback 9780385339667" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer822849160">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3868938427703530603">I really want to like this book. As an attorney, I really wanted to. I just couldn't bring myself to like it. Sorry, Mr. Grisham.<br /><br />This book could have been better without so much emphasis on the filing of documents with the courts. Grisham attempts to create drama with the main character filing motions here, there, anywhere to try to get his estranged grandfather off death row. Sorry, but filing documents and motions after document and motion really isn't as exciting as it sounds. Wait, it doesn'</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3868938427703530603">I really want to like this book. As an attorney, I really wanted to. I just couldn't bring myself to like it. Sorry, Mr. Grisham.<br /><br />This book could have been better without so much emphasis on the filing of documents with the courts. Grisham attempts to create drama with the main character filing motions here, there, anywhere to try to get his estranged grandfather off death row. Sorry, but filing documents and motions after document and motion really isn't as exciting as it sounds. Wait, it doesn't sound exciting at all. I'd guess about 150 pages are wasted on describing motions of this sort and that and how they get rejected all along the way. While it may paint an accurate picture of the life of a death row lawyer (I can't say it does or does not, but assuming it does), it really doesn't do much to create compelling conflict or move the plot forward.<br /><br />That said, the portions of the book spent on the family history and the family drama were definitely interesting. This is what carried a second star for me. The protagonist's discovery of the disturbing aspects of the death row inmate/grandfather and the other family drama were the highest parts of this book.<br /><br />There are other interesting characters that don't really go anywhere. The co-conspirator that happens to show up on about 10 pages of the novel. Beyond the original commission of the crime that sends the antagonist to death row, nothing more comes of this character despite showing up three or four more times. As you read, you keep wondering what this guy is going to do. In the few pages spent on his late life excursions to conceal the truth, he doesn't actually do much. You keep hoping for some conflict created by him, but nothing happens.<br /><br />Another waste of space character is the protagonist's firm's partner who decides to pursue the main character's termination because of a bunch of BS about the main character being "dishonest" and lying on his job application about some minutae about being related to Sam Cayhill - the antagonist on death row. This guy and this subplot add nothing but annoyance to the book.<br /><br />The ending is quite predictable... I'll just leave it at that.<br /><br />Don't waste your time on this book. Grisham is a good writer, and I'm sure he has a good book that I haven't read yet.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3868938427703530603">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

It’s a somber subject – death row.<br /><br />STORY BRIEF:<br />Three Ku Klux Klansmen plant a bomb in an attorney’s office. Two children are killed. Only one of the men (Sam) is caught, and he stays silent about the other two. It is years later. Sam is on death row, soon to be executed in “the (gas) chamber.” Adam is a young attorney with a large law firm up north. He gets permission from his firm to work on Sam’s case pro bono. He is Sam’s grandson. Adam and Sam now m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17687414534976537591">Not as entertaining as other Grisham books. It’s a somber subject – death row.<br /><br />STORY BRIEF:<br />Three Ku Klux Klansmen plant a bomb in an attorney’s office. Two children are killed. Only one of the men (Sam) is caught, and he stays silent about the other two. It is years later. Sam is on death row, soon to be executed in “the (gas) chamber.” Adam is a young attorney with a large law firm up north. He gets permission from his firm to work on Sam’s case pro bono. He is Sam’s grandson. Adam and Sam now meet for the first time. The story is mostly about their interaction and Adam’s attempts to stop the execution.<br /><br />REVIEWER’S OPINION:<br />This is my fifth Grisham book. It wasn’t as enjoyable as the others. I was frustrated that Sam wouldn’t help himself by giving up names of others. But I understood his motive. He feared for the safety of his relatives. The plot might be interesting for some, but I wasn’t entertained. I wanted a fulfilling or happy ending which I didn’t get. Readers who want to contemplate death penalty issues might like this.<br /><br />DATA:<br />Copyright: 1994. Setting: mostly 1967 and 1990 Mississippi. Genre: death row legal fiction.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17687414534976537591">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer512621830">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11314035693137343093">I remember writing a review about Grisham's The Runaway Jury, and saying that it may be his best book. No, I was kinda caught in the moment. The Chamber is Grisham's best book by a mile. The plot is amazing, the characters are painted with a genuine touch, the topic of the death penalty and racism adds irresistibly to this modern masterpiece. Some say that the mark of a great book is that it leaves you asking questions that stimulates critical thinking and makes you consider your beliefs. This i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11314035693137343093">I remember writing a review about Grisham's The Runaway Jury, and saying that it may be his best book. No, I was kinda caught in the moment. The Chamber is Grisham's best book by a mile. The plot is amazing, the characters are painted with a genuine touch, the topic of the death penalty and racism adds irresistibly to this modern masterpiece. Some say that the mark of a great book is that it leaves you asking questions that stimulates critical thinking and makes you consider your beliefs. This is what "The Chamber" did to me. This novel is a moral entanglement to issues that have haunted the law and the people from long ago. It makes you wonder where the fine line between justice and murder is drawn, whether an eye for an eye is justifiable, and what forgiveness is really all about. Grisham at his best.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_281664232">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/281664232" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7713825-evan-leach" class="left imgcol" title="Evan Leach"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1384621842p2/7713825.jpg" alt="Evan Leach" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/281664232?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 15, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7713825-evan-leach" class="user" title="Evan Leach">Evan Leach</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/8683769-the-chamber" title="Paperback " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=1990-1999" class="actionLinkLite">1990-1999</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=thriller" class="actionLinkLite">thriller</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=american-literature" class="actionLinkLite">american-literature</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7713825-evan-leach?shelf=novels" class="actionLinkLite">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer281664232">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer746440890148507374">I don't think this is the best Grisham, but it's sneaky good. Grisham gets painted as a drafter of courtroom procedurals, which at times he has been, but he's underrated as a writer able to highlight social justice/injustice. This book is very much in the spirit of <a title="A Time to Kill by John Grisham" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32542.A_Time_to_Kill">A Time to Kill</a>, in the sense that it focuses on the morality of the legal system as opposed to its machinations. This story of a former KKK member sentenced to death isn't perfect, but it's definitely memorable. <strong>3.5 stars</strong>.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer303634878">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15847156648172384934">This was an excellent fictional account of a man on death row. Regardless of your religious or moral beliefs, the details of the account will make you think about the use of capital punishment in the United States. While the main character was certainly not a sympathetic character in terms of his actions, you couldn't help but emphathize with his demise. The scene outside the prison was a study in human nature, with KKK members and Nazis on one side and praying nuns on another. Definitely a book</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15847156648172384934">This was an excellent fictional account of a man on death row. Regardless of your religious or moral beliefs, the details of the account will make you think about the use of capital punishment in the United States. While the main character was certainly not a sympathetic character in terms of his actions, you couldn't help but emphathize with his demise. The scene outside the prison was a study in human nature, with KKK members and Nazis on one side and praying nuns on another. Definitely a book worth reading.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1493709753">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1493709753" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/39009840-patrick" class="left imgcol" title="Patrick"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1421964109p2/39009840.jpg" alt="Patrick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1493709753?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 03, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/39009840-patrick" class="user" title="Patrick">Patrick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5355.The_Chamber" title="Paperback 9780385339667" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1493709753">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11979434059492480022">The Chamber examines and provides insight to the entire death penalty process from a number of perspectives: the person on death row, the family of that person, the lawyers who litigate on his or her behalf, the state employees who carry out the task and the courts that provide the oversight. The fictional characters are portrayed in a manner that they actually seem real and worthy of the reader’s concern. Reading this book is comparable to reading about the Titanic – you know the ship is going</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11979434059492480022">The Chamber examines and provides insight to the entire death penalty process from a number of perspectives: the person on death row, the family of that person, the lawyers who litigate on his or her behalf, the state employees who carry out the task and the courts that provide the oversight. The fictional characters are portrayed in a manner that they actually seem real and worthy of the reader’s concern. Reading this book is comparable to reading about the Titanic – you know the ship is going to sink / you are sure the prisoner will be executed in the chamber – but it is an interesting story and you are compelled to see it through to the very end. Besides the pros and cons of the death penalty, the narrative explores other issues that people often struggle with: depression, love, hate, loyalty, racial prejudices, alcoholism, legal ethics, religious beliefs, life, death and the entire human condition. The narrative is very well done and at times blends LOL humor with the gruesome realities of the situation. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11979434059492480022">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1465961839">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1465961839" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/47317783-bailey-wavrunek" class="left imgcol" title="Bailey Wavrunek"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" alt="Bailey Wavrunek" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1465961839?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 11, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/47317783-bailey-wavrunek" class="user" title="Bailey Wavrunek">Bailey Wavrunek</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5355.The_Chamber" title="Paperback 9780385339667" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1465961839">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1341474758253169617">The Chamber is about an old man, Sam Cahall, who was once apart of the Klu Klux Klan. He was sentenced to life in prison after accidentally killing two young boys, and debilitating a man in the bombing of a Jewish law office. While this event took place in 1968, the book mostly takes place in the 1990's, when Sam Cahall is about to be executed. The book then goes to a man who just graduated from law school, and is bound and determined to represent Sam Cahall during his last 24 days to live. The</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1341474758253169617">The Chamber is about an old man, Sam Cahall, who was once apart of the Klu Klux Klan. He was sentenced to life in prison after accidentally killing two young boys, and debilitating a man in the bombing of a Jewish law office. While this event took place in 1968, the book mostly takes place in the 1990's, when Sam Cahall is about to be executed. The book then goes to a man who just graduated from law school, and is bound and determined to represent Sam Cahall during his last 24 days to live. The young mans name is Adam Hall. He is Sam's grandson. The name is different because when Sam was convicted of murder, his son left and changed his name in order to not be recognized as the son of Sam Cahall. When Adam learned about his family history, it made him want to be a lawyer, so he could represent his Grandfather that he never met. Once Adam meets Sam for the first time, Sam already know who it is. He could just tell. After a struggle, Sam does end up letting Adam represent him. Right away, Adam goes to work. He starts filing as many claims and proposals as he can think of to try and grant his Grandfather a stay. For the next 24 days, Adam gives it his all to try and award Sam a stay. Also during this period though, Adam learns a lot about his family history. Although Adam went into this battle wanting to learn more, he ended up not wanting to know everything about his family. Two things I liked about this book are that it was about the death penalty,and that the ending was a surprise. One thing I didn't like about the book was that the days were kind of dragged on a little. For a while, the book got kind of boring, but I'm glad I stuck with it. I would recommend this book to anyone who like books about law, or anyone who likes to read in general. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1341474758253169617">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1456360797">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1456360797" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/17242507-isabelle-tottenham" class="left imgcol" title="Isabelle Tottenham"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Isabelle Tottenham" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1456360797?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 30, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/17242507-isabelle-tottenham" class="user" title="Isabelle Tottenham">Isabelle Tottenham</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/5355.The_Chamber" title="Paperback 9780385339667" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1456360797">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15002951919780851158"> Crude and slightly provocative, John Grisham has yet again provided a page-turning novel that will leave your eyes strained and fingers raw as your inability to avert your eyes increases. Twisted families, religion and bombings are all tied together in this suspenseful and dramatic book about a young lawyer lobbying pro bono for his exiled grandfather while simultaneously uncovering the truth about his family history. The Cayhall family members are known for their inherent racism and participat</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15002951919780851158">	Crude and slightly provocative, John Grisham has yet again provided a page-turning novel that will leave your eyes strained and fingers raw as your inability to avert your eyes increases. Twisted families, religion and bombings are all tied together in this suspenseful and dramatic book about a young lawyer lobbying pro bono for his exiled grandfather while simultaneously uncovering the truth about his family history. The Cayhall family members are known for their inherent racism and participation in the Klu Klux Klan, therefore it is no surprise when Sam Cayhall is suspected and eventually arrested for a horrifying bombing of a Jewish lawyer. Sentenced to death row, Sam is devastated and his family is shaken. His already distant son and daughter blame themselves for not taking part in the Cayhall Klansmen tradition and branching off to start their own lives. So much so that Cayhall's son, father of Adam Hall a recently graduated lawyer, commits suicide. It is at his funeral that Adam begins to uncover the truth behind his father's side of the family and information regarding his relationship to his grandfather and inmate Sam Cayhall. Seeking the truth, he sets out to represent and begin a relationship with his newfound grandfather, with only a month he hopes to save Sam from his imminent fate while also delving into his family's roots. As time wears on, the book begins a fast-paced suspenseful race towards the end. Grisham does a beautiful job of seamlessly fitting each revealing secret together, developing the storyline and characters. I spent a lot of time researching legal terms used in the book, but nonetheless I have no constructive criticism to give towards the way Grisham masterfully created this book. The intriguing family dynamics and characters were compelling enough to make you want to continue flipping the page, eager to read what was next to unfold. <br /><br />	The target audience for this book would primarily be aimed more towards adults who enjoy suspense, but I thoroughly enjoyed it and didn't find the comprehension too difficult. I would recommend to any teens or adults, interested in law or mystery and of course to John Grisham fans alike. 'The Chamber' may be the best of his pieces yet. It is captivating and written with precision and detail, absorbing the reader’s attention. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15002951919780851158">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5bswOsq4BT9v0+JQBLpcZetgE0otalFU8a3kZe73ZTCAbw/pOGoRxeMLlut627rXopfQU2S3Fv6bpOH3ImKTdQ==')})
  }
//]]&gt;
</script>

At the height of his reign, Pablo\'s multibillion-dollar operation smuggled tons of cocaine each week into countries all over the world. Roberto and his ten accountants kept track of all the money. Only Pablo and Roberto knew where it was stashed-and what it bought.  And the amounts of money were simply staggering. According to Roberto, it cost $2,500 every month just to purchase the rubber bands needed to wrap the stacks of cash. The biggest problem was finding a place to store it: from secret compartments in walls and beneath swimming pools to banks and warehouses everywhere. There was so much money that Roberto would sometimes write off ten percent as &amp;quot;spoilage,&amp;quot; meaning either rats had chewed up the bills or dampness had ruined the cash.  Roberto writes about the incredible violence of the cartel, but he also writes of the humanitarian side of his brother. Pablo built entire towns, gave away thousands of houses, paid people\'s medical expenses, and built schools and hospitals. Yet he was responsible for the horrible deaths of thousands of people.  In short, this is the story of a world of riches almost beyond mortal imagination, and in his own words, Roberto Escobar tells all: building a magnificent zoo at Pablo\'s opulent home, the brothers\' many escapes into the jungles of Colombia, devising ingenious methods to smuggle tons of cocaine into the United States, bribing officials with literally millions of dollars-and building a personal army to protect the Escobar family against an array of enemies sworn to kill them. A juvenile sentenced to serve any period longer than nine months in a state-managed facility.\n\nThis is the story of four young boys. Four lifelong friends.\n\nIntelligent, fun-loving, wise beyond their years, they are inseparable. Their potential is unlimited, but they are content to live within the closed world of New York City\'s Hell\'s Kitchen. And to play as many pranks as they can on the denizens of the street. They never get caught. And they know they never will. \n\nUntil one disastrous summer afternoon.\n\nOn that day, what begins as a harmless scheme goes horribly wrong. And the four find themselves facing a year\'s imprisonment in the Wilkinson Home for Boys. The oldest of them is fifteen, the youngest twelve. What happens to them over the course of that year -- brutal beatings, unimaginable humiliation -- will change their lives forever. \n\nYears later, one becomes a lawyer. One a reporter. And two have grown up to be murderers, professional hit men. For all of them, the pain and fear of Wilkinson still rages within. Only one thing can erase it. \n\nRevenge. \n\nTo exact it, they will twist the legal system. Commandeer the courtroom for their agenda. Use the wiles they observed on the streets, the violence they learned at Wilkinson. \n\nIf they get caught this time, they only have one thing left to lose: their lives.\n\nSLEEPERS is the extraordinary true story of four men who take the law into their own hands. It is a searing portrait of a system gone awry and of the people -- some innocent, some not so innocent -- who must suffer the consequences. At the heart of SLEEPERS is a sensational murder trial that ultimately gives devastating, yet exhilarating, proof of street justice and truly defines the meaning of loyalty and love between friends.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/721.John_Grisham">About John Grisham</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/721.John_Grisham"><img alt="721" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1413390525p2/721.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer207557691594739991">"Long before his name became synonymous with the modern legal thriller, he was working 60-70 hours a week at a small Southaven, Mississippi law practice, squeezing in time before going to the office and during courtroom recesses to work on his hobby—writing his first novel.<br /><br />Born on February 8, 1955 in Jonesboro, Arkansas, to a construction worker and a homemaker, John Grisham as a child dreamed of</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText207557691594739991">"Long before his name became synonymous with the modern legal thriller, he was working 60-70 hours a week at a small Southaven, Mississippi law practice, squeezing in time before going to the office and during courtroom recesses to work on his hobby—writing his first novel.<br /><br />Born on February 8, 1955 in Jonesboro, Arkansas, to a construction worker and a homemaker, John Grisham as a child dreamed of being a professional baseball player. Realizing he didn't have the right stuff for a pro career, he shifted gears and majored in accounting at Mississippi State University. After graduating from law school at Ole Miss in 1981, he went on to practice law for nearly a decade in Southaven, specializing in criminal defense and personal injury litigation. In 1983, he was elected to the state House of Representatives and served until 1990.<br /><br />One day at the DeSoto County courthouse, Grisham overheard the harrowing testimony of a twelve-year-old rape victim and was inspired to start a novel exploring what would have happened if the girl's father had murdered her assailants. Getting up at 5 a.m. every day to get in several hours of writing time before heading off to work, Grisham spent three years on A Time to Kill and finished it in 1987. Initially rejected by many publishers, it was eventually bought by Wynwood Press, who gave it a modest 5,000 copy printing and published it in June 1988.<br /><br />That might have put an end to Grisham's hobby. However, he had already begun his next book, and it would quickly turn that hobby into a new full-time career—and spark one of publishing's greatest success stories. The day after Grisham completed A Time to Kill, he began work on another novel, the story of a hotshot young attorney lured to an apparently perfect law firm that was not what it appeared. When he sold the film rights to The Firm to Paramount Pictures for $600,000, Grisham suddenly became a hot property among publishers, and book rights were bought by Doubleday. Spending 47 weeks on The New York Times bestseller list, The Firm became the bestselling novel of 1991.<br /><br />The successes of The Pelican Brief, which hit number one on the New York Times bestseller list, and The Client, which debuted at number one, confirmed Grisham's reputation as the master of the legal thriller. Grisham's success even renewed interest in A Time to Kill, which was republished in hardcover by Doubleday and then in paperback by Dell. This time around, it was a bestseller.<br /><br />Since first publishing A Time to Kill in 1988, Grisham has written one novel a year (his other books are The Firm, The Pelican Brief, The Client, The Chamber, The Rainmaker, The Runaway Jury, The Partner, The Street Lawyer, The Testament, The Brethren, A Painted House, Skipping Christmas, The Summons, The King of Torts, Bleachers, The Last Juror, and The Broker) and all of them have become international bestsellers. There are currently over 225 million John Grisham books in print worldwide, which have been translated into 29 languages. Nine of his novels have been turned into films (The Firm, The Pelican Brief, The Client, A Time to Kill, The Rainmaker, The Chamber, A Painted House, The Runaway Jury, and Skipping Christmas), as was an original screenplay, The Gingerbread Man. The Innocent Man (October 2006) marks his first foray into non-fiction.<br /><br />Grisham lives with his wife Renee and their two children Ty and Shea. The family splits their time between their Victorian home on a farm in Mississippi and a plantation near Charlottesville, VA.<br /><br />Grisham took time off from writing for several months in 1996 to return, after a five-year hiatus, to the courtroom. He was honoring a commitment made before he had retired from the law to become a full-time writer: representing the family of a railroad brakeman killed when he was pinned between two cars. Preparing his case with the same passion and dedication as his books' protagonists, Grisham successfully argued his clients' case, earning them a jur</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="207557691594739991">...more</a>

    </div>

And look at you. A fine young man with a beautiful<br />education and a bright future. Where in the world did I go wrong? What happened to me?<br />I've spent my<br />life hating people, and look what I have to show for it. You, you don't hate anybody. And<br />look where you're headed. We have the same blood. Why am I here?”</span>

  <h2><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6270580-the-accountant-s-story?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false" class="readable bookTitle">The Accountant's Story: Inside the Violent World of the Medellín Cartel</a></h2>

      <div>
        by <a href="/author/show/335550.Roberto_Escobar?a=7&amp;origin=related_works" class="authorName">Roberto Escobar</a>
      </div>

          <div class="smallText uitext darkGreyText">
            <span class="minirating"><span class="stars staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="12x12"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="12x12"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="12x12"></span><span class="staticStar p6" size="12x12"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="12x12"></span></span> 3.59 avg rating — 953 ratings</span>            — published 2009
          </div>

    <div class="addBookTipDescription">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer504529267273850505">"I have many scars. Some of them are physical, but many more are scars on my soul. A bomb sent to kill me while I was in a maximum security prison has made me blind, yet now I see the world more clearly than I have ever seen it before. I have lived a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText504529267273850505">"I have many scars. Some of them are physical, but many more are scars on my soul. A bomb sent to kill me while I was in a maximum security prison has made me blind, yet now I see the world more clearly than I have ever seen it before. I have lived an incredible adventure. I watched as my brother, Pablo Escobar, became the most successful criminal in history, but also a hero to many of the people of Colombia. My brother was loved and he was feared. Hundreds of thousands of people marched in his funeral procession, and certainly as many people celebrated his death." These are the words of Roberto Escobar-the top accountant for the notorious and deadly Medellín Cartel, and brother of Pablo Escobar, the most famous drug lord in history. At the height of his reign, Pablo's multibillion-dollar operation smuggled tons of cocaine each week into countries all over the world. Roberto and his ten accountants kept track of all the money. Only Pablo and Roberto knew where it was stashed-and what it bought.  And the amounts of money were simply staggering. According to Roberto, it cost $2,500 every month just to purchase the rubber bands needed to wrap the stacks of cash. The biggest problem was finding a place to store it: from secret compartments in walls and beneath swimming pools to banks and warehouses everywhere. There was so much money that Roberto would sometimes write off ten percent as "spoilage," meaning either rats had chewed up the bills or dampness had ruined the cash.  Roberto writes about the incredible violence of the cartel, but he also writes of the humanitarian side of his brother. Pablo built entire towns, gave away thousands of houses, paid people's medical expenses, and built schools and hospitals. Yet he was responsible for the horrible deaths of thousands of people.  In short, this is the story of a world of riches almost beyond mortal imagination, and in his own words, Roberto Escobar tells all: building a magnificent zoo at Pablo's opulent home, the brothers' many escapes into the jungles of Colombia, devising ingenious methods to smuggle tons of cocaine into the United States, bribing officials with literally millions of dollars-and building a personal army to protect the Escobar family against an array of enemies sworn to kill them.  Few men in history have been more beloved-or despised-than Pablo Escobar. Now, for the first time, his story is told by the man who knew him best: his brother, Roberto.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="504529267273850505">...more</a>

    </div>

  <h2><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24983750-stop-frisk?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false" class="readable bookTitle">Stop &amp; Frisk</a></h2>

      <div>
        by <a href="/author/show/5047869.Sheryl_Sorrentino?a=7&amp;origin=related_works" class="authorName">Sheryl Sorrentino</a><span title="Goodreads Author!">*</span>
      </div>

          <div class="smallText uitext darkGreyText">
            <span class="minirating"><span class="stars staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="12x12"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="12x12"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="12x12"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="12x12"></span><span class="staticStar p3" size="12x12"></span></span> 4.22 avg rating — 27 ratings</span>            — published 2015

    <div class="addBookTipDescription">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer15766245259525508219">As bouncer, it’s Paulie’s job to protect pole dancers, break up brawls, and pat down the illegal farm workers and drug dealers who patronize Insanidád, a roadside “gentlemen’s club” on the outskirts of Modesto, California. But Paulie won’t rest until</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15766245259525508219">As bouncer, it’s Paulie’s job to protect pole dancers, break up brawls, and pat down the illegal farm workers and drug dealers who patronize Insanidád, a roadside “gentlemen’s club” on the outskirts of Modesto, California. But Paulie won’t rest until he finds out who killed his brother, Lloyd, a young pharmacist gunned down in his prime. 
 
By day, Paulie inhabits a camper where he talks to Lloyd’s ashes, lusts after his ex-girlfriend, and indulges an unbalanced neighbor who believes her father’s spirit is haunting her. By night, Paulie fends off the cagey Colombian drug lawyer about to marry his beloved ex (the bartender) and take over her father’s club. Unearthing the guarded secret between his unhinged neighbor and soon-to-be boss leads Paulie down a calamitous path of jealousy, recklessness, and unexpected redemption. 
 
Sheryl Sorrentino’s fifth novel exposes strip club life through the eyes of a street-smart “everyman” struggling for closure and kinship.</span>
